This decision was justified by the reduction in the number of double pillars from seven to five and the consequent environmental benefits of such a solution for the valley.
In addition, comparative calculations performed by ŘSD stated that a composite steel-concrete structure should be a less expensive option.
But there was an error in the calculations.
It stated that the new composite steel-concrete structure would be only 397 m long.
In fact, it is 5% longer.
During the award of the contract for the border bridge, the ŘSD made serious mistakes.
It did not prepare the tender dossier, which would adequately describe the criteria for assessment and partial criteria for comparing tenders.
This error led to the evaluation and evaluation committee and the Director-General evaluating the tenders subjectively and in a problematic manner.
The unsuccessful tenderer challenged the results of the tender procedure.
A subsequent investigation by the Office for the Protection of Competition resulted in the construction contract being signed later than originally envisaged.
This meant that the summer construction season of 2004, after evaluating the bids, was wasted and the time set aside for construction was shortened.
The construction company agreed to build the bridge in a shorter time, but at the cost of a substantial increase in costs.
The offered procedure included double assembly of the bridge superstructure, a change in the construction of the pillars and the establishment and equipment of assembly spaces.
In addition, the construction company was not able to obtain the originally designed type of steel in the required time.
The new type of steel - at a price higher by about € 4 million, which had to be paid in full by ŘSD - made it possible to shorten the time for anti-corrosion protection, which subsequently made it possible to shorten the total construction time of the border bridge.
Compared to the net price of € 18.3 million offered in the tender, the total cost of building the border bridge will increase significantly due to the accelerated procedure and other adjustments in funding.
The reduced time set aside for construction work has led to a price increase of around € 5.9 million.
At present, the amount of net costs is around € 25.9 million, without taking into account the automatic increase in prices (as of October 2006).
Comparison of prices of bridges and tunnels
The two supreme audit institutions analyzed the prices of bridges on the D 8 and A 17 motorways in order to identify bridges whose price was too high and to compare the prices of bridges on the German and Czech sides.
The analysis was based on methods of mathematical statistics.
Furthermore, the tunnels on the D 8 were compared with similar tunnels on motorways in Germany (A 71, A 38 and A 4).
There are no similar tunnels on the A 17 motorway.
In preparing this part of the inspection, it was important to accurately identify the relevant prices.
The two Supreme Audit Institutions have established the following principles for comparing bridge prices: based on the actual price paid for the bridge without value added tax (VAT rates differ in the Czech Republic and the SNR and are subject to change); disregard prices for special bridge foundations; convert prices in CZK to € according to the rate of the European Central Bank valid as of 30 December 2005 (CZK 29 = € 1).
On D 8 and A 17 there are bridges of various constructions (steel-concrete, prestressed reinforced concrete, composite steel-concrete and steel) and various sizes.
In an effort to ensure the homogeneity of the examined structures, the bridges were divided into groups according to the type of structure.
Within these groups, the size of the bridge was also taken into account in the analyzes.
By far the most common type of structure was concrete bridges on both sides of the border (20 bridges on the D 8, 36 bridges on the A 17).
The analysis focused mainly on this group of bridges, as the size of the set allowed the use of mathematical-statistical methods with a high degree of reliability of the results.
The inspections revealed that the prices per m 2 of concrete bridges on D 8 and A 17 depend on the size of the bridge (its length, area): the larger the bridge, the lower the price per m2 of bridge structure.
For German bridges, the price dependence on size was stronger than for Czech bridges (see graphs below).
Two concrete bridges on the A 17 and four on the D 8 have been identified, the price of which is significantly higher.
To find these bridges, a method was used to determine atypical values ​​in a set of numbers, as well as a regression analysis taking into account the dependence of the bridge price on its size (to determine atypically expensive bridges, the standard distance from the regression curve is upwards).
However, information on the atypically high price of these bridges should only be considered as an indicator of which bridges to focus on.
In order to draw a generally applicable conclusion, the causes of the high price in each identified case should be analyzed.
One such cause may be inefficient administrative measures, or environmental protection requirements or other acceptable reasons.
Given that Czech concrete bridges are on average longer than German ones (see table below), the proven negative dependence of the price of a bridge on its length could lead to the assumption that Czech bridges should be cheaper.
The hypothesis of lower prices of Czech concrete bridges is also supported by lower labor costs in the construction industry in the Czech Republic compared to Germany.
On the contrary, the results of the analysis show that the prices of concrete bridges on the D 8 are higher than on the A 17.
During the joint negotiations, two possible causes of this situation were identified:
1.
In Germany, most bridges are awarded as separate contracts.
Such an award allows small and medium-sized regional companies to participate in the tender.
In the Czech Republic, many bridges are awarded as part of a contract for a part of a motorway route that is several kilometers long.
Small and medium-sized companies do not participate in such a competition because the contract is too large for them.
2.
In the Czech Republic there is greater variability in the construction of bridges than in Germany.
In this context, the SAO found that the type of bridge construction was decided long before its construction.
The decision is made at the time when the ŘSD translates the application for a zoning decision.
At this time, the ŘSD selects bridge types from several variants, but economy plays a smaller role in decision-making.
A general conclusion on the prices of bridges in the Czech Republic and Germany could be expressed only on the basis of a thorough analysis of the construction market in both countries, which, however, would go beyond the scope of this inspection.
The border bridge is the most expensive composite steel-concrete bridge on the D 8 and A 17.
In the case of tunnels, it was found that the prices of tunnels on the D 8 are twice as high as tunnels in Germany.
However, a comparison of the main construction and technical parameters of the D 8 tunnels with the tunnels in Germany did not show any significant difference.
Significant price differences could be attributed to the fact that there is no real competition in the Czech Republic in the field of tunnel construction.
However, the small number of tunnels did not allow general conclusions to be drawn.
Efficiency and effectiveness of the construction of the Dresden - Prague motorway
The independent Czech Republic was established in 1993.
In the same year, the government approved a concept for the development of the motorway network.
The D 8 motorway was to be gradually extended from Prague to the state border with Germany.
Section No. 0805 across the Bohemian Central Mountains was to be acquired before the border section No. 0807.
The Czech Republic acquires the D 8 motorway as part of program financing.
In the program No. 327 220 "Construction of the D 8 motorway Prague - Ústí nad Labem - state border of the Czech Republic / Germany" two goals were set for the construction of the D 8 motorway:
1.
to improve the connection between Prague and Ústí nad Labem - a national goal;
2.
to improve the connection between Prague and Berlin within the framework of IV. trans-European transport corridor Berlin-Istanbul - international destination.
According to the program, the construction of the section leading through the Bohemian Central Mountains was to be completed in June 2008 and the construction of the border section No. 0807 in June 2007.
It should be added here that the completion date is not identical to the commissioning date.
The SAO found that the preparation of the construction of the section leading through the Czech Central Mountains is now in the stage of an official zoning decision.
Environmental activists have challenged the decision with lawsuits.
There is no final decision on the actions.
Therefore, it cannot be ruled out that the section leading through the Bohemian Central Mountains will be completed several years later than stated in the program documentation.
In the summer of 2006, the Czech Ministry of Transport proposed postponing the completion date of section No. 0805 to 2010.
As a result of the above facts, the construction of the border section did not reach the national goal - improving the connection between Prague and Ústí nad Labem.
ŘSD performed an estimate of capital expenditures for the border section D 8 No. 0807 and section No. 0805 across the Bohemian Central Mountains in the years 1999 - 2001.
The estimated investment costs included in these capital expenditures are around € 639 million together for both sections 0805 and 0807.
Table 2: Results of the economic assessment (EIRR = internal rate of return)
All considered motorways and expressways open, both sections of D 8 opened in 2007.
In the northern part of the border section, the intensity of traffic was 18,000 vehicles per day in 2010
All considered motorways and expressways open, both sections of D 8 opened in 2007.
In the northern part of the border section, the intensity of traffic was 22,000 vehicles per day in 2010
All considered motorways and expressways open, both sections of D 8 opened in 2007.
In the northern part of the border section, the intensity of traffic was 25,000 vehicles per day in 2010
The economic analysis carried out in 2001 was based on the assumption that the border section and the section across the Bohemian Central Mountains will be completed on time.
At the time of the inspection, however, it is clear that the section across the Bohemian Central Mountains will not be open in the original term.
This will reduce the above EIRR values.
Costs for both sections excluding VAT, calculated as at 31 December 2005, were € 897 million, ie 27.4% higher than the level of costs used in the economic analysis in 2001.
However, there is a risk that section 0805 will be run on another, more expensive route, which would affect the above-mentioned economic viability threshold.
The economic analysis showed that the construction of both sections of the motorway is economically viable with a traffic intensity of at least 18,000 vehicles per day in 2010.
The forecast for 2008 was 22,940 vehicles, but a study addressing the issue of detours for the missing section through the Czech Central Highlands envisages a traffic intensity of 12,600 to 13,750 vehicles per day, which pushes the investment below the limit of economic viability.
With a higher load of detours for the missing section through the Bohemian Central Mountains, there is a high probability of congestion, and thus an increase in external costs, which were not taken into account in the original economic analysis.
The above-mentioned reduction in revenues and increase in costs means that the already low EIRR that the construction of the border section D 8 should have had will not be achieved.
Construction of the D 8 border section is therefore inefficient.
Also during the construction of the A 17 motorway, the costs were exceeded in a similar way as in the case of these two sections on the D 8.
As part of the overall economic assessment of the federal road development plan for long-distance transport for 1992, the Federal Ministry of Transport of Germany estimated the cost of around € 320 million.
The resulting cost / income ratio was then 4.6.
In the meantime, however, costs have risen sharply to around € 670 million, reducing the cost / income ratio by more than half.
In addition to the parallel inspection carried out jointly with the SAO, the BRH inspected the preparation and construction of the A 17 motorway in 2003 and 2005.
At the same time, the BRH found that the competent state approval authority required an off-road tunnel for the third section A 17, which was not needed.
During the inspection, the BRH succeeded in convincing the Federal Ministry and the German Road Construction Administration that a tunnel was not necessary.
The result was savings that amounted to around € 10 million in construction costs alone and € 0.15 million in annual operating costs.
During the assessment of the first section A 17 BRH found that the construction project was not properly prepared.
The following example illustrates this:
In the preparatory stage, the road construction administration did not carry out a survey of the bedrock conditions in the Dölzschen rocks, as the site was difficult to access.
This was done only after the tunnel boring machine hit a rock.
Tests have shown that the rocks are more weathered inside than expected during the tender.
Therefore, costly measures had to be taken, such as mooring, grouting and stone fall protection nets, which required around € 3.3 million.
The Directorate of Roads and Motorways had to bear the costs not only of other emergency operations, such as safety measures in the Dölzschen rocks, but also measures that would not be necessary if the construction project had been properly prepared first.
Expenditure of this kind includes costs related to the interruption of construction operations (€ 3.2 million), further training of drilling staff (€ 0.5 million) and rising wages and expenditure on goods due to the project being exceeded (€ 7 million).
The cost overrun thus amounted to approximately € 10.7 million, which did not correspond to any added value.
Based on the lessons learned from this and other audited projects, the BRH asked the Federal Ministry to apply stricter management in its supervision of road construction work.
At the same time, the Federal Ministry should give the Roads and Motorways Directorates the necessary impetus to implement quality assurance systems.
Benefits of cooperation for the BRH and the SAO The cooperation fulfilled the intention of both audit institutions.
BRH gained control knowledge about the preparation, assignment and course of construction of the border bridge.
The two Supreme Audit Institutions have set up a database of bridge and tunnel prices on which future inspections can be based.
The SAO found that the main construction and technical parameters of bridges, tunnels and the route of the D 8 motorway do not differ from the parameters of A 17.
The inspectors of both institutions participating in the cooperation gained new experience, which they can use in further inspections.

Coordinated control of funds intended for financing projects implemented under the Operational Program for Cross-Border Cooperation Czech Republic - Poland 2007–2013
INTRODUCTION
The developing community of the people of Europe is the driving force behind cross-border cooperation as an integral part of the wider territorial cooperation that has been part of our lives for the last 20 years.
Let us therefore give this community an introductory word on this joint report of the two supreme audit institutions of the neighboring countries:
"185 million EU citizens (37% of the population) live in border areas.
These areas are usually underdeveloped and peripheral areas, often a remnant of the darker parts of European history.
The main objective of cross-border cooperation is to help alleviate the existence of borders as administrative, legal and physical barriers and to further develop the dormant potential of border regions.
The programs cover different border regions: some cover maritime areas, others inland borders within the EU or borders shared with candidate countries.
Most programs are bilateral, but some include more than two neighboring countries. "
The programs run for seven years and receive a total of € 5.7 billion from the EU budget.
"The programs include the following activities: repairs and reconstruction of cross-border roads, cycle paths and bridges, investments in cross-border waste management systems, medical facilities, research centers, flood defenses, etc., joint management of natural sites or development of tourist destinations, development of joint services for local people, employment counseling, the creation of thematic networks and clusters for innovation.
Support from the program is provided on the condition that organizations from both sides of the border (such as regional authorities, universities or small and medium-sized enterprises) come together to implement projects that are based on the needs of the border region.
Representatives of the cooperating regions, relevant ministries and other local partners meet regularly to decide which projects will be supported.
This system should lead to a sustainable form of cooperation even after the end of the program itself.
Funding is provided on condition that the project partners on both sides of the border cooperate in a way that fulfills at least two of the following four characteristics: joint preparation, joint implementation, joint staff, joint financing. "
Within the partnership, the tasks and responsibilities for the preparation, implementation, financing and control of project activities are clearly defined and divided by the relevant partners.
The main attributes of the partnership include: joint choice of the lead partner, joint preparation of the project and application, conclusion of a joint partnership agreement signed by all partners, assumption of overall responsibility for project implementation by the lead partner, total support payments received by the lead partner and transferred to the relevant project partners.
Eligible projects implemented on the territory of regions and voivodships adjacent to the common border may be supported.
OBJECTIVES AND SCOPE OF CONTROL
On 11 December 2007, the European Commission (EC) approved the Operational Program for Cross-Border Cooperation Czech Republic - Poland 2007–2013 (OPPS ČR – POL).
€ 258,187,467 was allocated for the implementation of the operational program.
The total amount included a contribution from European Funds (ERDF) in the total amount of € 219,459,344 (85%), which must be used by the end of 2015.
Financial data on the drawing of EU funds from the Operational Program for Cross-Border Cooperation Czech Republic - Poland 2007–2013 at the end of 2013 are given in the following table:
The Agreement on Cooperation between the Supreme Audit Institutions of the Czech Republic (SAO) and the Republic of Poland (NIK) was concluded by the Presidents of both authorities on 27 February 2013 following previous mutual negotiations at the September 2012 in Lovasberény, Hungary.
The aim of the cooperation was to verify whether the implementation of the operational program is in accordance with legislation and binding programming documents or agreements, whether the management and control system works reliably, whether the objectives of the operational program are achieved and whether selected projects are implemented in accordance with the conditions. .
This co-operation took the form of a parallel audit, in which the audit authorities, on the basis of their statutory mandate, independently examined the topics, the content of which had been discussed together in advance.
THIS segment IS intentionally LEFT blank
The performed audit also included elements of a performance audit.
Selected topics were examined on a sample of projects at project partners on both sides of the border.
The result of the performed inspections were audit conclusions published in the usual way by both authorities.
This joint report is based on the two audit conclusions set out in the Annex.
The selected joint control sample included six projects (Annex 1), which fall into the area of ​​support 1.1 - Strengthening transport accessibility and the area of ​​support 2.2 - Support for the development of tourism.
Prior to the start of the audit, the drawing of funds from these two areas of support represented more than 60% of the funds paid to the beneficiaries by the end of 2012 from the ERDF for all implemented projects within the entire OPPS CR-POL.
THIS segment IS intentionally LEFT blank
In addition to the inspection of a joint sample of six projects, the SAO inspected another 13 projects and the NIK inspected another two (all from the area of ​​support 1.1 and 2.2).
The funds paid to the beneficiaries of ERDF support, which were audited by the SAO and the NIK, totaled € 25,362,174 (SAO) and € 8,450,784 (NIK).
The controlled volume of funds represented 41% of all funds paid to beneficiaries from support areas 1.1 and 2.2.
MAIN AUDIT CONCLUSIONS
Both inspections carried out independently by the SAO on the Czech side and the NIK on the Polish side showed that the level of identified irregularities did not exceed the level of materiality.
The implementation of the OPPS CR-POL, including the functioning of the management and control system and the implementation of selected projects, was successful.
The auditees, who were part of the management and control system, took steps to reduce the risk of misuse of funds and non-utilization of ERDF funds provided.
Proof of the effectiveness of the implemented projects within the operational program is the high level of fulfillment of the set objectives, which was also supported by the results of the survey of social evaluation of project implementation.
SUMMARY OF CONTROL RESULTS
A. Evaluation of the management and control system
The coordinated approach made it possible to control the management and control system of OPPS CR-POL implemented by the following institutions in both countries: the managing authority (Ministry for Regional Development of the Czech Republic), the national coordinator (Ministry for Regional Development POL) and controllers (Center for Regional Development and Voivodship).
At the same time, the coordinated audit enabled the evaluation of the tasks and functions performed by the Joint Technical Secretariat and the Monitoring Committee.
During the evaluation of the management system, the audit focused on activities carried out in connection with the setting up and performance of the function of managing authority and national coordinator, in particular in setting the program objectives and monitoring progress, evaluation activities and annual reports, assessments, evaluations, selection and approval project applications, drawing on program funds and fulfilling the obligation to publish correct, complete and up-to-date information on grants, as well as control activities of the managing authority, setting up activities and outputs from audits of the authorized audit entity and irregularities reporting and monitoring system. coordination of tasks and cooperation between institutions involved in the OPPS CR-POL.
Furthermore, the activities of the competent authorities in the area of ​​risk analysis and the compilation of on-site inspection plans were examined, and the evaluation of project applications carried out by independent evaluators was assessed.
The audit of the control system included a review of administrative and on-the-spot checks to approve, for example, deliveries of goods and co-financed services, eligibility of expenditure submitted for reimbursement and compliance of these funds and related activities (or parts thereof) with national legislation and legislation. EU.
Significant shortcomings were identified by the SAO's audit in the area of ​​publishing correct, complete and up-to-date information on the subsidies provided.
In the area of ​​project evaluation and approval, the risk of non-transparency of this process was identified.
Other shortcomings were found in the area of ​​setting monitoring indicators and in setting up the irregularity reporting system.
Based on these findings, the Supreme Audit Office assessed the control and management system implemented by the managing authority as partially effective.
The irregularities found by the NIK, which concerned the functioning of the management and control system in Poland in relation to breaches of applicable laws and regulations, were not assessed as significant.
These irregularities had an impact only on individual projects and did not have a significant impact on the implementation of the entire OPPS CR-POL.
Despite the identified discrepancies, the NIK assessed the functioning of the system positively.
B. Evaluation of project results
The audit did not show a discrepancy between the indicators and objectives set for individual projects and those defined by the operational program.
In all audited projects, the expected results were achieved, the financed activities were implemented and the indicators (measurable parameters) set out in the project applications of the operational program were met.
The audit conclusions are in accordance with the result of the survey of the social evaluation of the implementation of projects described below.
An important aspect to which the SAO and NIK paid attention in their conclusions is the limited possibility of objective evaluation of the fulfillment of the expected results of individual projects with regard to the objectives of individual projects and at the same time to the objectives of the entire operational program.
At the initiative of the Supreme Chamber of Control, a public opinion survey was conducted, which took place in the summer of 2013 at the place of implementation of projects selected for the joint control sample.
The survey and its evaluation were carried out by the School of Higher Vocational Education in Nysa (Poland).
Following an agreement with the Supreme Audit Office, the survey was conducted on both sides of the border between 2,820 respondents, both with the public in Poland in relation to part of the project implemented by Polish project partners and with the Czech public in relation to part of the project in the Czech Republic.
The survey mainly concerned the suitability, quality, exchange of information, use of funds and the social function of projects, including investments made.
The number of respondents for individual projects is given in Annex 2.
The assessment of the survey responses indicated that the overall social evaluation of the projects was good and reached a value of 4.8 points (on a scale with a maximum of 8 points), which means that project results were positively perceived by most respondents both in terms of objectives and other parameters. specified in the project applications.
According to the respondents, the examined projects met the criteria expressed in the operational program.
Respondents also noted the positive impact of investments on improving the quality of transport and tourism infrastructure, transport accessibility, tourist attractiveness, business conditions, the quality of life of citizens and the resumption of contacts between the inhabitants of the border region.
On the other hand, the overall evaluation of the responses indicated a certain difference between the actual level of fulfillment of the objectives / parameters (actual degree of impact) and the level expected by the respondents.
This difference applies to the evaluation of the results of individual projects, where in some cases there is a significant difference.
The results of the survey were in line with the opinions of participants in panel discussions (target groups) carried out within the NIK inspection on the Polish side, who pointed out the need for project implementation and confirmed their positive public acceptance and fulfillment of expected results.
C. Evaluation of the legality of the audited projects
The compliance of material and time implementation of the project with the schedule and content of the project application was checked, the fulfillment of set goals and purpose of the project, fulfillment of binding monitoring indicators, management of changes in projects, handling of possible project revenues, fulfillment of separate accounting records of project transactions, compliance with the ban on financing projects from other EU sources, implementation of the project partnership and compliance with its conditions, compliance with mandatory publicity concerning especially raising awareness of EU project financing sources, compliance with conditions of eligibility of project expenditures, selection of suppliers and verification of delivered contracts.
The total financial value of deficiencies qualified by the SAO and NIK as expenditure ineligible for EU funding was found to be € 85,094.
However, this amount did not exceed the set level of materiality related to the total volume of funds controlled for the sample of projects.
Despite the identified irregularities, the SAO and NIK evaluated the implementation of selected projects positively by the beneficiaries.
In most cases, the obligations arising from the conditions of the aid were properly fulfilled by the beneficiary.
Funds intended for the implementation of audited projects were spent in accordance with EU regulations and national law.
Nevertheless, irregularities in the fulfillment of material and financial obligations were found in seven of the eight beneficiaries controlled by NIK.
These were individual cases of a non-systemic nature and concerned in particular the inconsistent preparation of project documentation and breaches of public procurement rules, which, however, did not have an impact on the selection of the contractor or the delivery of the contractually agreed works.
Only in one of all projects audited by the NIK can a detected irregularity concerning non-compliance with reporting obligations be assessed as ineligible expenditure, amounting to PLN 43 003 (of which € 8,231 financed from the ERDF).
On the other side of the border, the SAO identified ineligible expenditure in the amount of CZK 2,098,053 in three of the 19 projects audited (of which € 76,863 financed from the ERDF).
Irregularities included non-delivery of paid material and works or expenditure on modernization of real estate owned by a person who was not linked to the project either as a beneficiary or as a project partner.
RECOMMENDATION
The joint recommendation of the SAO and the NIK concerns the improvement of the monitoring system, especially with regard to the consistent setting of proper monitoring indicators for all specific objectives of the priority axis of the operational program.
In general, it is also necessary to strive for the establishment of such indicators at the level of the program and projects, which will enable a more reliable verification of the fulfillment of the determined impacts.
The comments and recommendations resulting from the inspections carried out on both sides of the border are included in the separate audit conclusions of the SAO and the NIK on CD.
COMMUNIQUE
The report was prepared in Czech, English and Polish and signed by the presidents of the supreme audit institutions of the Czech Republic and the Republic of Poland.
Procurement of construction work and prevention of corruption
Joint report on parallel audits carried out by the Czech Supreme Audit Office (SAO) and the German Federal Court of Auditors (BRH)
Introduction and overview
Procurement of goods and services by contracting authorities is very important for both the public and private sectors.
The role of the state is to use public resources as efficiently and economically as possible and to ensure fair and regulated free competition.
In the European Union (EU), public procurement is a key factor in the success of the Single Economic Space.
The EU has set limits in its regulations above which a contracting authority must meet certain requirements to guarantee a wide and unrestricted competitive environment throughout the EU.
EU Member States must therefore transpose and put European procurement law into national law.
The Czech Republic and Germany have already done so.
For public contracts that are below the EU limits in value, the regulation of their award remains within the competence of individual countries.
In addition, public procurement is prone to corruption with consequent high potential for harm.
Therefore, contracting authorities are obliged to take reasonable measures in their procurement procedures to prevent corruption and unfair favoritism (prevention of corruption).
In January 2011, the Supreme Audit Office of the Czech Republic (Czech SAI - SAO) and the Federal Court of Auditors of Germany (German SAI - BRH) agreed to carry out parallel audits in the area of ​​procurement procedures and prevention of corruption in buildings and transport structures.
The cooperation was also focused on the area of ​​European procurement law as it was transposed into national legislation.
The inspections also included the award of contracts below European limits in order to cover the area of ​​corruption prevention.
The audit findings of the two SAIs are summarized in a joint report addressed, inter alia, to international organizations such as the International Organization of Supreme Audit Institutions (INTOSAI).
Areas of control and controlled institutions
The BRH assessment is based on comprehensive inspections of the above-mentioned areas, which were carried out by the German building administrations responsible for federal transport and civil engineering in the years 2011 to 2012.
For example, the construction of motorways and large-scale building construction projects were evaluated.
The BRH also included findings from previous inspections in the evaluation.
The SAO's evaluation is based on inspections carried out during the years 2008 to 2012, which focused on the construction of roads, railways, water projects, large buildings (hospitals, cultural facilities, universities) and some other structures in the Czech Republic.
In Germany, federal public procurement is mostly carried out by the state building administrations.
In the Czech Republic, state institutions for road and railway construction are responsible for awarding public contracts in the field of transport infrastructure.
In the field of building construction, the award of public contracts is carried out by entities that are recipients of budget funds.
In both countries, the services of external experts are also used in the individual phases of the procurement procedure.
The evaluation must take into account the differences in public procurement legislation and the specificities of the construction market in the two countries.
The structure of the law on public procurement in both countries
In Germany, the conduct of European procurement procedures is governed by laws and regulations.
Procurement procedures limited to Germany are governed by budgetary law and the relevant administrative acts.
The internal administrative rules contain numerous additional provisions for both European and national procurement procedures.
Further details concerning the requirements of European procurement law and the prevention of corruption are enshrined in the so-called federal government procurement manuals, in which the requirements are elaborated or formulated in greater detail with regard to legal precedents in the field of procurement law.
In the Czech Republic, the award of public contracts is governed by the Public Procurement Act and other legal regulations that apply to contracts with a value above and below European limits.
The contracting law underwent significant changes in 2012.
The new rules have strengthened the transparency of procurement procedures, opened up space for more competition and reduced opportunities for corruption during the procurement process.
Basic aspects of procurement law in both countries
As far as European procurement procedures are concerned, open and restricted procedures in particular need to be applied in both countries.
In Germany, contracts below the European threshold are awarded in accordance with national rules (public call for tenders, restricted call for tenders, negotiated procedure), but these are practically identical to the above conditions for European tenders.
Public procurement in Germany is characterized by the principle of preference for open procedures (where candidates are invited on a European scale) and public invitations to participate (only in the case of national procedures).
In open procedures and in the invitation to participate in the competition, the tender may be submitted by any interested party.
Another important feature of German procurement law is the support of small and medium-sized companies.
This is achieved primarily by dividing the total public contract into commercially specific parts and dividing it into partial contracts.
By awarding contracts divided into these parts, it is given a chance to participate in the procedure by as many interested parties as possible.
The two characteristics of German contract law are time-tested tools for obtaining good value for money.
More than 330,000 construction companies with 1.9 million employees are registered in Germany.
In the Czech Republic, it is necessary to apply the conditions of European procedures also in cases of awarding public contracts with a value below the EU limit.
An exception is the simplified sub-limit procedure, where the contracting authority may use less demanding procedures.
In accordance with European law, Czech procurement law includes the conditions under which a contract must be awarded in order to ensure competition and achieve a favorable price.
Current Czech legislation allows contracting authorities to choose between open and restricted procedures.
There are 85,000 registered construction companies in the Czech Republic, of which about 1,900 have 20 or more employees.
The total volume of contracting authorities' contracts for public works and the share of individual types of proceedings in both countries
In 2011, the total volume of construction work in Germany amounted to 7,675 billion.
CZK, of which CZK 1,138 billion (15%) accounted for the share of public contracts, which, in addition to contracts awarded by the federal government, also included contracts awarded by individual federal states and municipalities.
The total volume of federal road and building projects (excluding the construction of railways and waterworks) reached CZK 212.5 billion.
In 2011, the composition of federal contracts for civil engineering and transport construction was as follows: 80.7% open procedures and public calls; 12.7% restricted procedures and restricted calls;
6.6% negotiated procedures and direct award of contracts; 33.2% is the share of European tenders.
The volume of public construction contracts in the Czech Republic in 2011 reached CZK 118.6 billion, which was about 25% of the total volume of all construction works.
In 2009, it reached the value of CZK 194.0 billion, which was 37% of the total volume.
Structure of types of procurement procedures in 2011: 65.1% open procedures; 14.3% restricted procedure; 9.5% negotiated procedure; 8.4% simplified procedures for sub-threshold contracts; 2.7% other proceedings.
The figures include all public supply, service and works contracts awarded by contracting authorities in 2011.
These figures show that: parallel controls in both countries focused on a substantial part of public procurement procedures; the prevention of corruption, which must always be taken into account in procurement procedures, has a significant financial impact; open meaning. Procurement procedures are of key importance.
Summary of main results
The SAO and the BRH carried out parallel inspections of the award of European works contracts with an emphasis on the prevention of corruption in their countries.
The inspections focused on the comparison of legislation in both countries and the EU and on the implementation of public procurement in the field of land and transport construction.
Here are the key findings:
The EU Public Procurement Directive has been transposed into the national law of both countries (points 1 and 3.1).
Prevention of corruption is not only the goal of the EU directive, but the national procurement rules of both countries, which differ in part in terms of focus and detail.
In both countries, the basic requirement that the tender dossier must meet is the inclusion of all the information needed to prepare tenders.
The specification must be neutral and must not contain any requirements restricting competition.
The tender documents in Germany generally meet these requirements.
In the Czech Republic, the problem is the insufficient definition of the subject of the contract.
In Germany, the division of contracts into lots is required where possible in order to promote a competitive environment.
According to the findings of the BRH, this requirement is complied with in Germany.
The SAO's inspections have shown that in the Czech Republic, considerable volumes of public works are awarded in the field of road construction, without being divided into parts, or they are divided, but only into very significant parts in terms of volume. This can distort competition and lead to restrictions on only a few large companies.
These limitations on market potential can result in significant cost increases and create the risk of frivolous price negotiations and inadmissible agreements.
Legislation in both countries allows contracting authorities, under certain conditions, to subcontract further construction work if this need arises only after the conclusion of the contract (point 4.1).
In both countries, the requirements for the correct determination of the type of procurement procedure are met.
In the Czech Republic, according to the valid regulations, contracting authorities are free to determine one of the open procurement procedures, namely open procedure or restricted procedure.
German procurement and budgetary law gives priority to open procedures for both European tenders and national tenders, known as public invitations to tender.
Open procurement procedures in both countries allow a wider range of bidders to submit a bid and also reduce the risk of corruption, as they make it difficult to negotiate prices illegally between bidders.
At the same time, the best results are achieved in the area of ​​value for money.
In the case of large contracts whose estimated total price reaches or exceeds the limits set by the EU, the audited contracting authorities always issued a European procurement procedure.
In determining the type of procurement procedure, the correct determination of the estimated value of the contract plays a very important role.
In Germany, databases are used to objectively determine the price of public works, in which the values ​​are based on completed construction projects.
This is a sufficiently objective tool.
In the Czech Republic, databases are used only in the field of road construction, but they are not yet an effective tool (Section 4.2).
When awarding a works contract under European procurement procedures, it is necessary to publish prior information and a call for tenders in order to strengthen competition by enabling candidates to prepare in time for tenders.
As a rule, the construction authorities audited complied with this requirement.
In isolated cases, errors in the information released, incorrect application of provisions and / or a lack of controls may have impaired competition (item 4.3).
There is a greater risk of manipulation when selecting candidates for participation in a restricted tender procedure.
Under EU law, the number of candidates invited must be high enough to guarantee real competition.
The selection criteria must be objective, non-discriminatory and published well in advance.
In addition to EU requirements, contracting authorities in both countries should, as often as possible, rotate candidates who are invited to participate in the selection process when selecting suppliers.
In Germany, the requirements for risk prevention are partly very detailed.
In some cases, the audited contracting authorities did not comply with these requirements.
In the Czech Republic, selection decisions were not always credible (section 4.4).
In both countries, contracting authorities must ensure during the procurement procedure that all candidates wishing to submit a tender receive the same information.
Any agreements between these candidates and between them and third parties involved in the preparation of the specifications must be avoided as far as possible so as not to restrict competition by means of illegal agreements.
In Germany, the relevant measures are more thorough than in the Czech Republic.
However, the German contracting authorities inspected did not comply with this requirement in several cases (point 4.5).
In both countries, the principle of cross-checking and transparency must be applied when opening envelopes.
The requirements for labeling, documentation, protection and confidentiality of tenders are intended to limit the scope for manipulation and are particularly detailed in Germany.
Inspections in both countries found only a few cases of irregularities (section 4.6).
The assessment and evaluation of tenders is procedurally regulated in both countries.
The relevant German measures are more complex, but have not always been respected by the inspected contracting authorities.
For example, contracting authorities have forwarded their tenders to third parties for inspection without prior assessment, without duly documenting any suspicious circumstances.
The tenders were thus insufficiently protected against subsequent manipulations (point 4.7).
Due to the legal protection of the enterprises, the participants must be informed about the intended award of the contract before the latter is finalized.
This legal protection rule is also designed to prevent corruptive action.
The law of the Czech Republic provides for judicial review of contract award procedures also for national tenders.
Only in a few cases, the notification of the enterprises was not appropriately ensured (item 4.8).
The tender documentation was not always complete or completely credible for the audited contracting authorities in both countries.
In the case of several procurement procedures, the transparency requirements applied at European level were not always properly respected in the minutes.
In addition, some of the identified shortcomings reduced the preventive anti-corruption function of the documentation.
Contracting authorities in both countries mostly fulfilled their reporting obligations (section 4.9).
Summary of the main principles of European procurement law and prevention of corruption
European contract law
To ensure free competition in the EU, the European Parliament and the Council of the European Union have adopted, inter alia, a directive on the coordination of procedures for the award of public works contracts, public supply contracts and public service contracts to ensure the free movement of goods and services and a directive on the coordination of laws, regulations and administrative provisions relating to review procedures. in the award of public supply contracts and public works contracts (Review Procedures Directive), the two directives were to be transposed into national law.
The EU's general procurement principles include in particular: the requirement for a competitive environment; the requirement of equal treatment or non-discrimination; transparency requirement.
EU procurement principles include The Directive on the coordination of procedures for the award of public contracts also includes provisions for construction projects with a total estimated value of € 5 million or more.
These provisions affect: the content of the tender dossier; the type of procurement procedure used; disclosure of certain information before, during and after the proceedings; meeting the deadline for receipt of tenders; qualification requirements that may be required of candidates; criteria for selecting the most suitable tender.
The legal protection of candidates is governed by the Review Procedures Directive.
In the event of a potential breach of the contract award law, tenderers may have the contracting authority's decision reviewed in accordance with the examination procedures.
Both countries have transposed EU requirements into national legislation.
Corruption prevention
The importance of corruption prevention
In neither country is there a legal definition of what corruption actually means.
Corruption is generally understood as the abuse of personal (confidential) status for one's own benefit.
As a rule, this means that such abuse is encouraged by those who benefit.
Corruption can hinder competition and cause serious economic losses.
It is estimated that the total damage related to corruption amounts to 5% of world economic output.
For the EU, these costs are estimated at 1% of the EU's economic output.
If a contracting authority is involved in the contract, conventions restricting competition or allowing unfair practices may undermine confidence in the integrity, ability and overall work of the public administration.
Therefore, the prevention of corruption is a crucial task for contracting authorities.
Practices and procedures must be monitored, especially in areas particularly prone to corruption, so that adequate measures can be taken to prevent them.
Taking preventive measures and carrying out effective controls can significantly reduce the vulnerability of the decision-making process to corruption.
Procurement of construction works is generally considered to be a particularly vulnerable area to corruption.
Measures in the field of corruption prevention
In Germany, corruption can be prosecuted under criminal and labor law, in particular active and passive bribery, acceptance and offering of significant benefits.
The German federal government has issued a manual with recommendations concerning the prevention of corruption in the federal administration, according to which the following principles are applied throughout the procurement process outside the risk analysis: the priority of open procurement; the principle of multiple control and transparent decision-making; planning, procurement and accounting phase departments; accurate and complete documentation of the tender procedure.
In addition, other measures having a preventive effect against corruption have become part of the procurement law.
In 2011, the relevant Federal Ministry introduced information technology to support a system for monitoring the award of contracts for the construction of federal roads.
With this system, data such as estimated costs, total contract value and involvement of private design offices can be fully recorded and analyzed to identify possible irregularities.
In the Czech Republic, corruption is prosecuted under criminal law, and the level of labor or civil law is also possible.
The legal requirement for transparency, equal treatment and the prohibition of any discrimination during the entire procurement process (preparation and implementation) is the most important part of preventing corruption.
Anti-corruption measures in this area were set by the government in the "Government Strategy for the Fight against Corruption for the Period 2011 to 2012", which was updated for 2013 and 2014.
Part of the mentioned measures was incorporated into the Public Procurement Act and related implementing regulations.
The new legislation contains the following basic provisions: reduction of limits for small-scale contracts; the obligation of prior notification by the contracting authority, including the reasons for the contract; a ban on limiting the number of candidates in restricted procedures and the abolition of the general possibility to limit the number of candidates by drawing lots; the obligation to publish contracts, including annexes; obligation to publish actual and lists of subcontractors.
Results of controls related to the subsequent stages of the procurement procedure
Tender documentation
The tender dossier must contain all the information necessary to enable tenderers to prepare their tenders in an appropriate form.
In particular, the documentation must make it possible to process the offer price.
For this to be possible, tenderers must not be exposed to risks which they could not have foreseen.
The tender documentation contains mainly the specifications, as well as the requirements for proving the qualifications of the tenderers and the criteria for selecting the most advantageous tender.
Requirements of European law and prevention of corruption
European law contains, in particular, requirements concerning technical specifications.
The specification must restrict competition as little as possible and all interested parties must have equal access to it.
The specification must be neutral.
Requirements for specific products are permissible on a very limited scale in the tender documents.
The basic principle of corruption prevention is that the tender documentation does not contain requirements that could restrict competition.
The documentation should also avoid ambiguities or inaccurate data, which create room for speculation and manipulation in the evaluation of tenders.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Both countries have transposed EU legal provisions into their national legislation.
The basic principle is that specifications are generally given without reference to a specific product or brand.
The specification must specify the supplies and services required and specify the numbers of units of work and supplies.
In normal cases, prices should be quoted by units of work or supply (eg in CZK / m or in CZK / item).
Defining the required works in terms of their functionality is permissible only in exceptional cases.
In principle, the rule is that the specification must be drawn up completely and unambiguously.
They must contain the exact quantities and must not include works and supplies the necessity of which is not entirely clear.
Reimbursement in the form of lump sums or time worked should be avoided where possible.
These parts of the tender dossier must preclude speculation and manipulation based on ambiguous specifications.
The specifications must enable each tenderer to prepare his tender with confidence and without undue preparatory work.
The invitation to tender must be published only when the tender dossier is complete.
Different provisions
In the Czech Republic, there is no obligation for contracting authorities to award construction contracts in installments.
The contracting authority may divide the public contract into several separate parts, if the nature of the subject of such a contract allows it.
In Germany, the application of the rule of dividing works into parts is required, either quantitatively (partial deliveries) or according to specific considerations (specialized parts of the contract).
This allows small and medium-sized entities to participate in the competition, increases the number of potential bidders and makes it difficult to fix prices.
This type of award is usually considered the most appropriate in terms of providing value for money.
In the Czech Republic, the tender documentation must contain evaluation criteria for national and European tender procedures.
In this way, the tenderer should be comprehensively and exhaustively informed of the criteria according to which his tender will be evaluated.
In Germany, the requirement to include evaluation criteria in the notification only applies to European invitations to tender.
In national procurement procedures, it is only necessary to include evaluation criteria in the contract record, which is an internal administrative document.
If the tender dossier is prepared by authorized independent experts, German regulations require the exclusion of any information that would help to identify the specific companies, architects or designers involved.
The reason is to prevent individual construction companies from obtaining, without the knowledge of the contracting authority, any additional information or other unacceptable competitive advantages through contacts with these independent experts.
This is also the reason why independent experts must not publish tender documents and make them accessible for scrutiny.
The contracting authority is the only point of contact for construction companies.
The Czech Republic does not currently have a similar provision.
In the case of large construction projects, after the conclusion of the original contract, the need for additional work or modification of existing work will almost always arise.
The legislation of both countries allows the award of a contract for extra work up to a certain limit to the same contractor without the need to publish and carry out a new procurement procedure.
The volume of additional work according to European and German legislation may not exceed 50% of the original value of the contract.
In addition, additional work must be necessary and must be in the nature of a specific part of the main contract.
In the Czech Republic, a stricter limit of 20% is applied for the submission of additional work.
This can bring the risk of a longer time delay in the implementation of the contract and the possible incorporation of a new entity into an existing project.
If the overtime contract is awarded to the supplier of the original contract, German law contains a provision which prevents suppliers from arbitrarily increasing the price of modified works or overtime.
Calculations must be made taking into account the prices agreed in the original contract.
In addition to overtime, there may be fewer jobs during construction, ie work that was not needed.
In the Czech Republic, less and more work cannot be compensated for each other.
Main control findings
BHR
Technical specifications and product-neutral call for tenders
In rare cases, the specifications referred to national technical requirements or standards, but have in the meantime been replaced by EU standards.
In some cases, the specifications contained data requiring a specific trademark, eg for a public sign resistance system, to prevent water penetration or for noise cladding, although this was not required.
In some cases, the specifications were so detailed that only one product met the requirements.
Quantitative specifications divided into items
In not a single case of control of procurement procedures did the BRH record any non-compliance with the general requirements for the call for tenders for supplies and services according to the quantitative specifications divided into items.
Completeness of specifications
In most of the cases audited, the tender documents were fully drawn up before the call for tenders was issued.
In various cases, however, the documents had to be modified during the procurement procedure to take account of changes in items or timetables.
In rare cases, the tender documentation contained work and supplies whose need was uncertain.
One tender documentation for demolition work contained almost exclusively such items.
Division of the contract and criteria for awarding the contract
In many cases, the required justification for not dividing supplies into parts has not been demonstrated.
BRH did not find any shortcomings in the criteria for the evaluation of tenders before the award of the contract.
Inclusion of the names of authorized independent experts
In many documents, interested authorized experts were mentioned.
The names were mentioned on the introductory pages of the documents or on the plans or attached opinions.
In one case, the contracting authority even invited tenderers to contact the explicitly appointed architect with their questions about the tender procedure.
In the case of building projects, these cases were found mainly in the tender documents, where self-employed experts were entrusted with the elaboration of the specifications.
The contracting authorities stated that they did not have sufficient staff capacity for a detailed examination of the tender documents.
SAO
Technical specifications and product-neutral call for tenders
In the case of a public contract for the construction of a house with 12 apartments, the reference was directly to specific manufacturers and brands of products.
The contracting authority did not allow the use of other similar types of products nor did it justify why it required the delivery of specific products.
In the construction of university facilities, the tender documentation contained references to specific business companies and specific product designations.
General overview of specifications and partial specifications
For the construction of some industrial zones, the contracting authority did not clearly define in the tender documentation the quantity and type of required works, supplies or services in the statement of acreage and determined for each building as a unit of measure only the enclosed space in cubic meters.
Completeness of specifications
During the tender procedure for the public contract for the construction of the ring road, the tender documentation for the restricted procedure was changed four times from August to September 2009.
The reason was changes in the project documentation caused by the extension of the scope of the contract and the change in technical conditions.
In the construction of social facilities, the tender documentation had defects that forced the emergence of additional work (there was no precise focus, there was not enough construction and technical research, there were no drawings).
In the tender documentation for the construction of a large library, the interior elements were mentioned only descriptively, without specifying the qualitative requirements for the products.
The supply of the interior equipment of the building was provided by subcontractors who purchased some above-standard and very expensive equipment.
Parts and criteria for the award of the contract
In the Czech Republic, there is no obligation to award a contract in installments.
The analysis of three large road constructions during the construction of the ring road showed that when they were awarded in an open procedure in parts (separately for selected buildings), it was possible to achieve savings of up to CZK 790 million from the achieved public procurement price of CZK 15,508 million.
For example, when six buildings were awarded as a separate part of the public contract, savings of 62.5 mil.
CZK, ie 25% of the contracted price, and when 20 bridges were awarded as a separate part of the public contract, savings of CZK 287.8 million, ie also 25%, could be achieved.
During the construction of roads, the contracting authority set qualification criteria in the tender documentation, which limited the participation of small suppliers in the tender procedure.
In the tender conditions of the open tender for the contractor for one motorway construction, the contracting authority required, among other things, the contractor's practice of implementing at least three road constructions with a financial volume of more than CZK 700 million, completed or put into operation during the last five years.
There are only nine companies in the Czech Republic that meet the condition of completing constructions over CZK 700 million in the last five years.
The public contract for the supply of another motorway construction in an open procedure included six separate constructions, for which the contracting authority required a minimum annual volume of construction work in the last three years of CZK 12 billion.
Two thirds of the financial volume of road construction works (ie € 2.5 billion out of € 3.7 billion) in the period of 2008–2009 were realized by 5 suppliers or individually either in form of consortium.
Of the 46 contracts for a total of CZK 13.2 billion, there were only two or even one bidder.
The SAO pointed out that a reduction in market potential could lead to a large increase in costs.
Area of ​​multiple works
During the construction of the ring road, the contracting authority did not conclude a written contract for the supply of additional work for the construction of the construction in two cases and did not award the additional work to any of the procedures specified in the law.
The value of unapproved extra work in one case was CZK 1,591.4 million excluding VAT (an increase of 21%) and in the other case CZK 4,135.8 million excluding VAT (an increase of 91%).
The reason for the extra work was mainly the ambiguous definition of the amount and type of work required.
In the construction of university facilities, the contracting authority did not include in the tender documentation some works and supplies, the necessity of which for the completion and approval of the construction was already known when awarding the contract.
For these works and supplies, he concluded amendments to the work contract with the construction contractor with an increase in price, in a negotiated procedure without publication, even though the volume of additional work exceeded the national limit of 20% of the value of the original contract.
In connection with the construction of the Palace of Justice, the contracting authority reserved the right to award additional work without a tender procedure.
During the construction, the contracting authority increased the scope of the contract by CZK 50.5 million.
The unit prices of the additional works determined without competition were significantly higher than comparable works performed by the same supplier.
Other findings
On a selected sample of constructions, the SAO found that the average construction costs for the construction of one kilometer of a four-lane motorway in the Czech Republic are CZK 355.0 million.
According to long-term data obtained from the German Ministry of Construction, the average cost in Germany per kilometer of the four-lane motorway is CZK 260.0 million.
This comparison suggests that the acquisition of a motorway in the Czech Republic is more expensive than in Germany.
Summary
The legal provisions concerning the tender dossier are almost identical in both countries and are in line with the requirements of European law.
The aim is to ensure full and equal competition and prevention of corruption.
The specifications must be sufficiently precise to enable interested parties to calculate costs and prepare their tenders.
In addition, this requirement limits the scope for speculation and the contracting authority is protected against the need to outsource work.
In Germany, the basic legal provisions on tender documents are complemented by other rules which are intended to further reduce the scope for restricting competition and manipulation.
Although there are no detailed requirements of this kind in the Czech Republic, greater transparency is achieved by publishing award criteria.
In both countries, the requirements for specifications are usually met.
However, in some cases, shortcomings were identified which could lead to a restriction of competition.
The current rule in Germany, which requires the division of large construction projects into parts, seems appropriate to strengthen the competitive environment and achieve cost-effective results.
The SAO found that in the Czech Republic, the award of contracts for large-scale projects without the award of lots or the division into large-scale lots may limit the range of applicants to a small number of large suppliers.
The result of the strict requirement for tenderer references, which is applied in the Czech Republic for the selection of tenderers for road construction work, is the fact that public contracts are awarded only to a small circle of tenderers, and small and medium-sized companies do not have a chance to compete.
The limited number of bidders also increases the risk of price fixing and other illegal conventions.
Regulatory approaches are different in the two countries with regard to the award of additional works contracts or modified works and their remuneration.
If the value of the additional work under European and German law does not exceed 50% of the value of the original contract, the extra work may be awarded without a call for tenders.
In the Czech Republic, a stricter limit of 20% applies.
THIS segment IS intentionally LEFT blank
This limit is often exceeded by the contracting authority.
THIS segment IS intentionally LEFT blank
If the 20% limit is exceeded, overtime must be awarded on the basis of a new procurement procedure.
By dividing the work into parts, it is possible to avoid restricting competition and the consequent unfortunate consequences.
Determining the type of procurement procedure with regard to EU limits
By determining the type of procurement procedure, the contracting authority defines the range of potential suppliers that will be admitted to the competition.
The contracting authority may leave the scope of potential suppliers unlimited, or it may limit the scope according to specified criteria.
In the case of large project works whose value reaches or exceeds the limits set by the EU, a European procurement procedure must be conducted.
Requirements of European law and prevention of corruption
When choosing the type of procedure, the contracting authority must comply in particular with the requirements of European law concerning the competitive environment and transparency.
If the estimated total cost of the contract for the required works reaches or exceeds the current EU limit of € 5.0 million (CZK 125 million), an open or restricted tender is always required at European level.
In exceptional cases, a negotiated procedure or a competitive dialogue is permissible.
If the construction project reaches the EU limit, the contracting authority may deviate to a limited extent from the European procurement procedure by issuing a national procedure if the total value of the relevant part is below € 1.0 million (CZK 25 million) and the total value of all parts does not exceed 20% of the estimated value of the whole contract.
Dividing a construction project or deliberately estimating its costs too low in order to avoid the fundamental obligation to launch a European tender is not permissible.
The choice of the type of proceedings is very important for the prevention of corruption, because restricting competition significantly increases the risk of manipulation.
Therefore, an important part of the tender documentation is a detailed justification of the chosen type of procedure, especially in relation to the obligation to apply European procurement law.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Both countries have fully implemented the EU's legal requirements for procurement and limits in their national legislation.
Different provisions
The Czech Republic has introduced the principle of equal validity of open and restricted procedures also for procurement procedures at the national level.
Open and restricted procedures can always be chosen, while other types of procedures are allowed only if the conditions stipulated by law are met.
Unlike the Czech legislation, the relevant German legislation on budgetary law and the principles of prevention of corruption prefers open procedures or a public invitation to participate in proceedings.
Other types of proceedings, including restricted proceedings, are permitted only in exceptional cases, if permitted by law.
Such exceptions must be justified and documented.
The principle of open procedure preference is intended to ensure that restrictions of competition are avoided as far as possible and that opportunities for manipulation are limited (pre-selected specific candidates).
In Germany, this principle of public procurement is central to the prevention of corruption.
A correct estimate of the expected contract price is an important basis for deciding on the appropriate type of procurement procedure.
In estimating the costs of building projects, contracting authorities in Germany can rely on the budget and cost databases of public administrations and other organizations.
These have been compiled on the basis of an analysis of completed construction projects and include, inter alia, standard cost values ​​for types of construction, building units, building parts and partial construction work.
Based on such standard cost values, it is possible to estimate the contract value of a planned project or construction work.
Only a few building administrations in Germany already have a similar database for road construction.
However, the database is expected to be expanded at national level.
Contracting authorities in the Czech Republic do not use any central database or average prices of comparable objects to determine the expected costs of public works contracts.
In many cases, the determination of costs is based on an estimate of the entity that finances the project or on the value of the resources available to that entity.
Standardization measures are still at an early stage.
Based on the anti-corruption program for the period 2011 to 2012, the relevant ministries are to develop a database of usual prices of standardized commodities.
The use of the price system to determine the expected price of the contract is determined by a new regulation ordering the subject of the contract to be specified in detail.
The pricing system should serve as a set of information on construction and assembly work, materials and products, and other data in order to calculate the necessary costs and to determine unit prices.
The Ministry of Transport has issued normative prices for transport constructions for the purpose of their control, which are regularly updated.
The database is intended for determining the price of a project (investment) plan and should play a comparative function in the next stages of preparation of construction work.
The main control findings of BRH
Selection of the tender procedure
The audited contracting authorities generally awarded contracts in an open procedure or in the form of a public call for participation, thus complying with the requirements of budgetary and procurement legislation.
In 2011, the share of public tenders in the total volume of federal building projects in the field of building construction was 59.5%.
In road construction, this share was 94.8%.
BRH found that in some cases the use of non-public competition was not sufficiently justified.
In the case of a comprehensive inspection, the BRH found, on the basis of information gathered from more than 16,000 proceedings, that a restricted procedure has a clear disadvantage compared to a public procedure and a public call, and that restricted procedures also increase the risk of manipulation and corruption.
THIS segment IS intentionally LEFT blank
According to the BRH study, tender prices in restricted and negotiated procedures were 6% to 10% higher than in public procedures.
Parallel studies by the Ministry of Construction and the Provincial Audit Office confirmed differences in value for money obtained in public and restricted proceedings.
A study by the Ministry of Construction even found that prices in restricted proceedings were twice as high as prices in public proceedings.
Compliance with EU limits
For projects with a total contract value estimated at more than € 5 million (CZK 125 million), the audited contracting authorities usually prepared procurement plans stating which works would be awarded in the European procurement procedure and which in the national procedure.
According to the tender plans, the share of works awarded in accordance with European rules always exceeded 80% of the total work.
No case of circumvention of the European management requirement has been reported in the form of an excessively low cost estimate.
However, in some cases the minimum share for pan-European management was not reached due to a calculation error.
During the execution of the works, some contracting authorities illegally changed the procurement plan.
They did not justify these changes and did not demonstrate that the minimum share of European management would subsequently be respected.
The share of European procedures according to the project accounts was generally lower than the share set out in the tender plan.
If the expected values ​​of construction project contracts exceeded the European limits, the contracting authorities did not apply the European procedure to all individual partial parts in excess of € 1 million (CZK 25 million).
Instead, they awarded the relevant contracts on the basis of a national procedure, without giving strong reasons for the need to derogate from the requirement.
SAO
Selection of the type of tender procedure
In the case of several large construction projects (eg construction of a road section, equipment for a major sporting event and construction of flats), the contracting authority divided one project into several parts, although these sub-contracts were of the same or similar nature.
The division reduced the expected value of the contract and the contracts were awarded in a less demanding regime or even outside the statutory regime.
Methods of calculating the estimated price
Price standards applicable to road construction projects have not yet been an effective tool for determining the estimated price.
They were based on the prices quoted in the completed proceedings, which, however, were burdened by inefficiencies and excessive profits of suppliers.
The SAO found that the actual prices for the three parts of the ring road were about 37% lower than these price standards.
The Ministry of Transport is to update the standards in relation not only to past prices, but also in comparison with other comparable markets and price developments.
For buildings, the estimated price was based on the project budget made by the architects or the investor's estimate.
The actual price then differed significantly from the estimated price.
The estimated cost of building a large library was twice as high as the contract price, but increased by CZK 54 million, ie by 27%, during implementation.
Similarly, for the construction of the depository of this library, the contract price was 33% lower than the expected price.
In other cases, the price of the successful applicant was higher than the expected price and increased even further during implementation.
In the construction of a large library, it was by CZK 400 million, ie by 22%. In the construction of the Palace of Justice, the actual price increased disproportionately during the construction (by 139%) compared to the increase in functional areas (only by 12%).
Summary
Both countries have implemented European provisions on the choice of the type of procurement procedure and on respecting European limits into their national legislation, so that the respective national regulations of both countries are almost identical.
However, the principle of public proceedings in Germany is stricter than the European regulation and the current national regulation in the Czech Republic.
